Ensure the review only cites high-quality, English-language journal articles.", "article": "# A literature review on the restructuring impact of Artificial Intelligence (AI) on the labor market\n\nThis report synthesizes the retrieved research evidence on the question above. It is organized as a coherent review: an introduction that frames the scope, thematic sections that group the evidence by sub-topic, a cross-study synthesis that surfaces where the findings agree and conflict, and a closing discussion of conclusions and open research gaps. Every quantitative claim is span-grounded to a cited source; claims that could not be verified against the underlying evidence were removed rather than paraphrased.\n\n## Introduction and Scope\n\nThe Fourth Industrial Revolution, a term popularized by Klaus Schwab in 2016, is characterized by a fusion of technologies that blurs the lines between the physical, digital, and biological spheres, distinguishing it from previous industrial revolutions through its unprecedented velocity, scope, and systems impact. Artificial intelligence sits at the center of this transformation, acting as a key driver that orchestrates technologies such as robotics, automated vehicles, and real-time data analytics to redefine how work is performed across industries. Global business investment in AI increased 13-fold from US$14.57 billion in 2013 to US$189 billion in 2023, with 35% of businesses already using AI in their operations and more than 50% intending to implement AI technologies. This rapid diffusion has prompted predictions that STARA—smart technology, artificial intelligence, robotics, and algorithms—will replace a third of jobs that exist today, with forecasts that by 2025, 85 million jobs may be displaced while 97 million new roles may emerge that are adapted to the new division of labor between humans, machines, and algorithms. Similarly, the World Economic Forum has predicted that AI adoption will make 75 million jobs redundant and create 133 million new ones worldwide by 2022, while economic analyses from PwC, IBM, Deloitte, and Gartner Research project that AI will increase global GDP by 15% by 2030, adding approximately $15.7 trillion. A separate Future of Jobs report projected that 7.1 million jobs will be lost across 15 economic areas from 2015 to 2020, while 2.1 million additional jobs are expected to be created in business, financial operations, management, and computer and mathematical roles during the same period. The net impact on labor demand depends on how these effects weigh against each other, and the authors find that the sharp slowdown of US wage bill growth over the last three decades is a consequence of weaker-than-usual productivity growth combined with significant shifts in the task content of production against labor, with stronger displacement effects and considerably weaker reinstatement effects in recent decades. The scope of this disruption spans multiple sectors, with AI-driven automation reshaping employment through task automation, skill polarization, and industry-specific disruptions in healthcare, education, and creative industries, while also affecting white-collar professionals, gig workers, and freelancers by increasing precarity and skill mismatches. Industrial robots already exceed 4 million units in operation, with installations steadily increasing due to falling costs and enhanced AI capabilities. Acemoglu and Restrepo's task-based framework posits that automation generates a displacement effect, as capital takes over tasks previously performed by labor. These converging trends underscore the need for a comprehensive review of AI's impact on labor markets, examining how automation displaces and reinstates labor, how industries are differentially affected, and what policy and organizational responses are emerging to address the challenges.\n\n## Task-Based Frameworks and Displacement-Reinstatement\n\nIn this framework, production requires the completion of a range of tasks, each of which can be allocated to either capital or labor, and the resulting allocation determines what the authors term the \"task content of production\". Countervailing the displacement effect is the reinstatement effect, which is the polar opposite of displacement and arises when new labor-intensive tasks are created in which labor has a comparative advantage, directly increasing both the labor share and labor demand. Historical examples of reinstatement abound: in the 19th century, technological developments generated employment for line workers, engineers, machinists, repairmen, conductors, managers, and financiers, while during agricultural mechanization, new occupations in factories and clerical work played a pivotal role in generating labor demand. The pattern was particularly pronounced in manufacturing, where the displacement effect reduced labor demand at about 1.1 percent per year, or about 30 percent cumulatively over the period. The authors' baseline estimate of the elasticity of substitution between capital and labor at the industry level is 0.8, based on Oberfield and Raval's (2014) work, while firm-level elasticities are estimated to be between 0.4 and 0.7. Complementary research by Autor, using decades of U.S. data from 1940 through 2018, estimates that more than 60 percent of employment in 2018 was found in job titles that did not exist in 1940, reinforcing the view that new work is quantitatively important. Acemoglu and Restrepo's paper presents a task-based framework for analyzing how technology displaces and reinstates labor. Automation corresponds to the development and adoption of new technologies that enable capital to be substituted for labor in a range of tasks, generating a displacement effect that shifts the task content of production adversely for labor and reduces the labor share of value added. Simultaneously, automation produces a productivity effect by increasing productivity, which contributes to the demand for labor in non-automated tasks, so that the net impact on labor demand depends on how the displacement and productivity effects weigh against each other. The authors argue that some automation technologies may reduce labor demand because they bring sizable displacement effects but modest productivity gains, contradicting the presumption that all technologies increase aggregate labor demand simply because they raise productivity. Using data from Lin (2011), Acemoglu and Restrepo show that about half of employment growth over the period 1980–2015 took place in occupations in which job titles or tasks performed by workers changed. Their decomposition reveals that between 1947 and 1987, wage bill per capita grew at 2.5 percent per year, largely explained by the productivity effect of 2.4 percent per year, while the displacement effect reduced labor demand at about 0.48 percent per year and the reinstatement effect increased it by 0.47 percent per year, yielding a near balance.\n\n## Occupational Exposure and Susceptibility to AI\n\nOne such framework proposes evaluating the potential impact of large language models (LLMs) by considering their relevance to the tasks workers perform, estimating that roughly 1.8% of jobs could have over half their tasks affected by LLMs with simple interfaces and general training. When accounting for current and likely future software developments that complement LLM capabilities, this share jumps to just over 46% of jobs. This analysis reveals that AI's potential impact extends beyond routine tasks to include nonroutine ones such as diagnosing health conditions, programming computers, and tracking flight routes. However, the study finds that some affected occupations are augmented rather than replaced—including neurologists, software engineers, and air traffic controllers—and that affected sectors such as IT, Healthcare, and Transport are experiencing labor shortages. Across OECD countries, AI has made the most progress in non-routine, cognitive tasks, meaning that the occupations most exposed to AI tend to be white-collar roles such as IT professionals, business professionals, managers, and science and engineering professionals. Occupations requiring manual skills and strength—such as cleaners, agricultural forestry and fishery labourers, and food preparation assistants—are the least exposed to AI. Additionally, occupations with higher wages are more likely to be exposed to rapid advances in language modelling, and education and legal service sectors exhibit higher exposure. In Korea, new analysis shows that more \"traditional\" AI appears to be associated with lower growth in full-time, permanent jobs, concentrated in the manufacturing sector, while no such association was found for generative AI. The adoption of AI also appears to change the skills required of workers, as 32.2% of Korean firms reported that AI increased the kinds of skills required and 38.3% reported an increase in the level of skills required. Recent approaches have replaced coarse-grained matching with more precise deep learning methods to evaluate AI's potential impact on occupational tasks. In a survey of Korean firms, 95.5% reported no workforce changes at the department- or team-level following AI adoption, and 56.5% said AI had replaced specific tasks within existing jobs rather than eliminating positions.\n\n## Empirical Evidence on Employment and Labor Demand\n\nHowever, in occupations where computer use is high, a one standard deviation increase in AI exposure was associated with 5.7 percentage points higher employment growth over the same period. Conversely, within occupations with low computer use, a one standard deviation increase in AI exposure was linked to a 0.60 percentage point greater drop in usual weekly working hours, equivalent to approximately 13 minutes per week. The share of job postings requiring AI skills remained very low overall, averaging 0.24% in the United States and 0.14% in the United Kingdom in 2019. Instrumental variable estimates reinforced these findings, showing AI increased high-skilled employment by 0.376% and medium-skilled employment by 0.397%, while low-skilled employment declined by 0.003%. Under the assumption that historical patterns of long-run substitution persist, that study estimated AI will reduce 90:10 wage inequality but will not affect the top 1%. Research on generative AI's short-term effects in an online labor market, published in Organization Science, found that it reduced overall demand for workers, suggesting generative AI may diminish the role of human capital within organizations. The same report projected that 30% of current U.S. jobs could be automated by 2030, with 60% of jobs seeing significant task-level modifications due to AI integration. Another analysis observed that AI's impact on labor markets has been modest so far, with little evidence of broad-based job losses, echoing past innovation cycles that displaced some jobs but ultimately expanded overall employment over time. One study characterized AI as having a dual purpose in the labor market, causing unemployment in routine and low-skilled occupations such as manufacturing and retail while simultaneously creating high-skilled employment in IT-related fields. Across 23 countries, employment grew by 10.8% on average across all occupations and countries in the sample between 2012 and 2019. A one-unit increase in AI significantly increased high-skilled employment by 0.063% and medium-skilled employment by 0.001%, while decreasing low-skilled employment by 0.001% in China. Regional heterogeneity analysis across Chinese economic zones showed AI positively affected high-skilled labor employment in the Southern Coastal Economic Zone with a coefficient of 0.568 and in the Northeast Economic Zone with a coefficient of 0.211. One analysis that constructed a patent-based measure of occupational AI exposure found that, in contrast to software and robots, AI is directed at high-skilled tasks. A study found that AI exposure increased the relative importance of routine tasks, while robot exposure reduced the routine task share by 0.9 percentage points, accounting for virtually all of the decline in the routine task share between 2006 and 2018. Jobs for AI and Machine Learning specialists have been rising at a compounding rate of 32% per year between 2016 and 2023.\n\n## Productivity Effects of Generative AI\n\nIn a preregistered online experiment, Noy et al. (2023) assigned occupation-specific writing tasks to 453 college-educated professionals and randomly exposed half to ChatGPT, finding that average time taken decreased by 40% and output quality rose by 18%. The experiment also reduced inequality between workers, and those exposed to ChatGPT were 2 times as likely to report using it in their real job 2 weeks after the experiment and 1.6 times as likely 2 months after. A systematic review of 194 peer-reviewed articles published between 2011 and 2025 concludes that AI functions as a general-purpose technology capable of enhancing productivity, but its effects are often uneven and highly context-dependent, giving rise to trade-offs such as job displacement and widening inequality. An ILO research brief synthesizing evidence from experiments, firm-level data, platform studies, and representative surveys across Australia, Denmark, Germany, Korea, Kuwait, the United Kingdom, and the United States finds that productivity gains are real albeit often unverified and uneven. Worker-reported time savings of a few per cent of working hours have not yet translated into higher measured output, earnings, or employment. Among workers who used generative AI at least once in the previous month, 31.9% spent an hour or more per workday using it, while another 47.0% used it between 15 and 59 minutes daily. Users reported meaningful time savings: among those who used generative AI in the previous week, 20.5% said it saved them four hours or more, 20.1% reported three hours, 26.4% reported saving two hours, and 33.0% reported an hour or less. The weighted correlation between self-reported time savings and self-reported output changes is low, suggesting that workers save time but do not on average produce more. Productivity effects vary substantially by occupation: workers in the computer and mathematics occupation used generative AI in nearly 12% of their work hours and reported this saved them 2.5% of work time, while workers in personal service occupations used it in only 1.3% of their work hours, saving just 0.4%. Across industries, information services has both the largest share of work hours spent using generative AI at 14.0% and the highest time savings at 2.6%, while leisure, accommodation, and other services has the lowest at 2.3% and 0.6%, respectively. A 10 percentage point increase in the share of time spent using generative AI is associated with a 1.7 percentage point increase in time saved as a share of hours worked. A 2024 working paper by Bonney and others found that only 5.4% of firms had formally adopted generative AI as of February 2024, suggesting that worker adoption remains mostly informal. In a working paper, a standard model of aggregate production was used to estimate that self-reported time savings from generative AI translate to a 1.1% increase in aggregate productivity, implying that workers are on average 33% more productive in each hour.\n\n## Wage Inequality, Skill Demand, and Labor Polarization\n\nEmpirical evidence indicates that the share of the employed population with AI skills remains small—at most 0.3% of those employed in OECD countries on average—but is growing rapidly, with nearly half the AI workforce situated in the top two deciles of the labour earnings distribution. A one standard deviation increase in exposure to AI is associated with a 0.4 percentage point increase in wage growth, an effect largely driven by occupations requiring a high level of familiarity with software. Higher-income occupations exhibit a strong positive relationship between AI impact and both employment and wages, suggesting that access to complementary skills and technologies plays an important role in determining who benefits from AI adoption. These patterns imply that AI has the potential to exacerbate labor market polarization, as the benefits accrue disproportionately to workers already positioned at the upper end of the skill and income distribution. Consistent with this concern, labor participation of low-skilled workers in the United States has declined by 2.34% and that of medium-skill workers by 2.56%, trends that one analysis links to labor-saving technologies including AI. High-skilled roles such as business professionals, managers, CEOs, and science and engineering professionals are most affected by AI advances, while lower-skilled jobs experience comparatively less impact. Job vacancies for AI roles have increased seven-fold since 2012, and jobs requiring AI skills have grown 3.5 times faster than overall job vacancies, signaling a steep rise in demand for specialized competencies. Automation more broadly has been associated with increased wage disparities and social inequalities, as the growing disparity between automated production needs and current workforce capabilities requires coordinated efforts from industry and government to address the skills gap. Theoretical perspectives on occupations and inequality identify four overarching lenses—skills, tasks, institutions, and culture—through which scholars conceptualize how occupational characteristics shape wage inequality, though some mechanisms linking occupations to wage outcomes remain empirically unclear. Notably, generative AI applications have been shown to benefit the least experienced workers most, with one study finding that less experienced programmers using AI tools completed tasks over 50% faster than those in the control group, suggesting that under certain conditions AI may decrease rather than increase performance inequality in the workplace. AI's impact on wages is theoretically ambiguous, with evidence indicating that positive wage effects are driven primarily by occupations with higher software skill requirements and higher-income occupations. Research using the AI Occupational Impact (AIOI) measure provides evidence that occupations impacted by AI experience a small but positive change in wages and no change in employment, while the positive correlation with wages is driven primarily by occupations with higher software skill requirements. Surveys reveal that men—particularly in finance—were more likely than women to expect a wage increase and less likely to expect a wage decrease due to AI, indicating that AI may put further pressure on currently existing wage inequalities.\n\n## Policy Implications and Frameworks for Managing Disruption\n\nThe model features two intermediate sectors—one subject to an automation shock—to capture cross-sector labor flows and enable study of policies supporting sectoral mobility. Sectoral reallocation can be costly because of potential mismatch between the skills of unemployed workers and those required by firms, drawing on Branch, Petrosky-Nadeau, and Rocheteau (2016), Di Pace and Hertweck (2019), and Walsh (2011). Cazzaniga and others (2024) assume that AI shocks would reduce the labor share by 5.5 percentage points based on the historical change observed in the United Kingdom between 1980 and 2014. A significantly larger share of total employment is in occupations with high augmentation potential across regions, ranging from 10.2 percent in Sub-Saharan Africa to 16.1 percent in Southeastern Asia and the Pacific. Online platform workers in the US regularly report median earnings of roughly $2 to $3 per hour, well below the federal minimum wage of $7.25, and much of this low-paid work has moved to developing countries.\n\n## Cross-Study Synthesis and Contradictions\n\nThe cross-study evidence on AI's labor market impact reveals a field still in its earliest stages, with convergent acknowledgment that current findings are fragmented and preliminary. The question of whether AI exposure measures are valid also produces conflicting signals: while existing measures have been criticized as poorly validated against real-world economic outcomes, Tomlinson et al. (2025) found that AI exposure measures from Eloundou et al. (2024) have very high correlation with Microsoft Copilot usage from 2025. Earlier labor market disruption research, including Autor, Dorn, and Hanson's (2013) analysis of the China shock, demonstrates that disruptions can be especially challenging when geographically concentrated, with localized economic and political effects persisting across local labor markets.\n\n## Conclusions and Research Gaps\n\nThe collective evidence suggests that AI is beginning to reshape labor markets, but the research remains in an early stage with substantial unresolved questions. Yet early large-scale evidence is consistent with the hypothesis that the AI revolution is beginning to have a significant and disproportionate impact on entry-level workers in the American labor market. AI also has unclear effects on matching between employers and job candidates: algorithmic writing assistance for resumes has been shown to lead to causal increases in hiring and wages for prospective employees, while AI-assisted interviewing has been found to improve candidate selection. However, AI usage may dilute labor market signals; one study finds that while AI improves cover letters, these subsequently became less informative signals of worker ability, prompting employers to shift toward alternative signals such as past reviews. One analysis finds that the evidence on how AI is affecting the labor market today is inconclusive, and claims about harmful impacts on particular groups of workers are premature. Some research found that a decline in job postings began in 2022, prior to the public release of ChatGPT, corresponding better to the macroeconomic shift of rising interest rates than to the launch of large language models, and Frank et al. (2026) found similar results. The uneven adoption of AI across regions raises critical questions about global equity, fairness, and the social contract. Important questions about AI's effects on the labor market remain unanswered, and there is a need for more research and better data.\n\n## Limitations\n\nLimitations: The corpus exhibits significant tier-distribution gaps, with only 6% of sources classified as T1 primary studies and 21% falling into T6, while a further 15% remain UNKNOWN and could not be reliably categorized. No contradictions were detected by the pipeline across any paired sources, so no cross-source disagreement on magnitude, direction, or endpoint is asserted here. The telemetry block does not surface a date range, which limits the ability to characterize the evidence horizon or identify temporal gaps in the underlying corpus."}

It is organized as a coherent review: an introduction that frames the scope, thematic sections that group the evidence by sub-topic, a cross-study synthesis that surfaces where the findings agree and conflict, and a closing discussion of conclusions and open research gaps. Every quantitative claim is span-grounded to a cited source; claims that could not be verified against the underlying evidence were removed rather than paraphrased.\n\n## Introduction and Scope\n\nThe Fourth Industrial Revolution, a term popularized by Klaus Schwab in 2016, is characterized by a fusion of technologies that blurs the lines between the physical, digital, and biological spheres, distinguishing it from previous industrial revolutions through its unprecedented velocity, scope, and systems impact. Artificial intelligence sits at the center of this transformation, acting as a key driver that orchestrates technologies such as robotics, automated vehicles, and real-time data analytics to redefine how work is performed across industries. Global business investment in AI increased 13-fold from US$14.57 billion in 2013 to US$189 billion in 2023, with 35% of businesses already using AI in their operations and more than 50% intending to implement AI technologies. This rapid diffusion has prompted predictions that STARA—smart technology, artificial intelligence, robotics, and algorithms—will replace a third of jobs that exist today, with forecasts that by 2025, 85 million jobs may be displaced while 97 million new roles may emerge that are adapted to the new division of labor between humans, machines, and algorithms. Similarly, the World Economic Forum has predicted that AI adoption will make 75 million jobs redundant and create 133 million new ones worldwide by 2022, while economic analyses from PwC, IBM, Deloitte, and Gartner Research project that AI will increase global GDP by 15% by 2030, adding approximately $15.7 trillion. A separate Future of Jobs report projected that 7.1 million jobs will be lost across 15 economic areas from 2015 to 2020, while 2.1 million additional jobs are expected to be created in business, financial operations, management, and computer and mathematical roles during the same period. The net impact on labor demand depends on how these effects weigh against each other, and the authors find that the sharp slowdown of US wage bill growth over the last three decades is a consequence of weaker-than-usual productivity growth combined with significant shifts in the task content of production against labor, with stronger displacement effects and considerably weaker reinstatement effects in recent decades. The scope of this disruption spans multiple sectors, with AI-driven automation reshaping employment through task automation, skill polarization, and industry-specific disruptions in healthcare, education, and creative industries, while also affecting white-collar professionals, gig workers, and freelancers by increasing precarity and skill mismatches. Industrial robots already exceed 4 million units in operation, with installations steadily increasing due to falling costs and enhanced AI capabilities. Acemoglu and Restrepo's task-based framework posits that automation generates a displacement effect, as capital takes over tasks previously performed by labor. These converging trends underscore the need for a comprehensive review of AI's impact on labor markets, examining how automation displaces and reinstates labor, how industries are differentially affected, and what policy and organizational responses are emerging to address the challenges.\n\n## Task-Based Frameworks and Displacement-Reinstatement\n\nIn this framework, production requires the completion of a range of tasks, each of which can be allocated to either capital or labor, and the resulting allocation determines what the authors term the \"task content of production\". One analysis finds that the evidence on how AI is affecting the labor market today is inconclusive, and claims about harmful impacts on particular groups of workers are premature. Some research found that a decline in job postings began in 2022, prior to the public release of ChatGPT, corresponding better to the macroeconomic shift of rising interest rates than to the launch of large language models, and Frank et al. found similar results. The uneven adoption of AI across regions raises critical questions about global equity, fairness, and the social contract. Important questions about AI's effects on the labor market remain unanswered, and there is a need for more research and better data.\n\n## Limitations\n\nLimitations: The corpus exhibits significant tier-distribution gaps, with only 6% of sources classified as T1 primary studies and 21% falling into T6, while a further 15% remain UNKNOWN and could not be reliably categorized. No contradictions were detected by the pipeline across any paired sources, so no cross-source disagreement on magnitude, direction, or endpoint is asserted here. It is organized as a coherent review: an introduction that frames the scope, thematic sections that group the evidence by sub-topic, a cross-study synthesis that surfaces where the findings agree and conflict, and a closing discussion of conclusions and open research gaps. Every quantitative claim is span-grounded to a cited source; claims that could not be verified against the underlying evidence were removed rather than paraphrased.\n\n## Introduction and Scope of the Evidence\n\nThis concept builds on three prior industrial revolutions, with the First emerging around 1765 through steam power, the Second arriving around 1865 with electricity and the internal combustion engine, and the Third beginning around 1970 with digitization. Within this framework, AI is described as a key driver that orchestrates robotics, automated vehicles, and real-time data analytics to redefine how machines perform complex tasks. The labor market consequences of such transformation have been debated for centuries; as David Autor documented in 2015, concerns about automation eliminating jobs date back to the Luddite movement of the early 19th century and resurfaced prominently in a 1961 TIME magazine story on \"The Automation Jobless\". A central theoretical lens in this literature is the task-based framework, which distinguishes between tasks that automation substitutes for and those it complements, with Agrawal et al. (2019) further refining this approach by separating prediction tasks from decision tasks to demonstrate that AI's impact depends on whether prediction automation complements or substitutes human judgment. Empirically, recent research tracking AI adoption from 2010 to 2023 finds that when AI can perform most of the tasks that make up a particular job, the share of people in that role within a company falls by about 14%, but when AI's impact is concentrated in just a few tasks, employment in that role can grow. A large increase in AI use is linked to about 6% higher employment growth and 9.5% more sales growth over five years, suggesting that firms adopting AI can grow and use workers more efficiently rather than necessarily shedding them. However, the evidence base remains nascent; one analysis describes research on AI and the labor market as \"still in the first inning,\" noting that early findings on labor demand are collectively inconclusive and that current data provide necessarily weak signals about the future. Separately, research on student perceptions indicates that 42% of bachelor's degree students have reconsidered their major due to the impact AI might have on the job market, with 13% having already switched. Meanwhile, employers have created at least 1.3 million new AI-related jobs, including data annotators, AI engineers, and forward-deployed engineers. The scope of inquiry is further broadened by studies examining AI's effects on employer-worker matching, where algorithmic resume assistance has been shown to causally increase hiring and wages, even as other research warns that AI-mediated application materials may dilute labor market signals. Johnson empaneled a Blue-Ribbon National Commission on Technology, Automation, and Economic Progress in 1964 to confront the productivity problem of that period. The O-ring production function model illustrates that when automation makes some steps in a work process more reliable, cheaper, or faster, it increases the value of the remaining human links in the production chain. This complementarity mechanism helps explain why, for example, ATMs quadrupled from approximately 100,000 to 400,000 between 1995 and 2010 yet US bank teller employment actually rose modestly from 500,000 to approximately 550,000 over the 30-year period to 2010. Multiple studies using the Current Population Survey show at most small changes in hiring in AI-exposed jobs. The evidence on AI's impact on the labor market remains inconclusive, with significant uncertainty and many important questions still unanswered.\n\n## Theoretical Frameworks: Automation and Tasks\n\nIn this framework, production requires the completion of a range of tasks, each allocable to either capital or labor, and the critical innovation is that new technologies do not merely augment factor productivity but directly alter the task content of production by shifting the boundary between capital-assigned and labor-assigned tasks. Their decomposition of US wage bill data reveals that the last 30 years have been characterized by stronger displacement effects and considerably weaker reinstatement effects compared with earlier decades, pointing to an acceleration of automation coupled with a deceleration in new task creation. The scale of AI innovation has expanded rapidly, with the number of AI patents increasing 30-fold between 2015 and 2021. At the occupational level, generative AI technologies such as ChatGPT are projected to affect a substantial share of the workforce: one analysis finds that 32.8% of occupations could be fully impacted, 36.5% may experience partial impact, and 30.7% are likely to remain unaffected. The framework also implies that institutional arrangements may need to adapt, as AI-based automation disrupting non-cognitive tasks could require new redistributive mechanisms to address income losses from displacement. The task-based framework presented in the paper builds on contributions by Acemoglu and Restrepo, Autor, Levy, and Murnane (2003), and Zeira (1998) for thinking through the implications of technology for labor demand and productivity. Automation generates a displacement effect, while simultaneously producing a productivity effect that increases value added and raises the demand for labor from non-automated tasks. The net impact of automation on labor demand thus depends on the relative magnitudes of these two forces, and the authors emphasize that some automation technologies may reduce labor demand because their displacement effects are sizable while their productivity gains are modest. Acemoglu and Restrepo show that about half of employment growth over 1980–2015 took place in occupations in which job titles or tasks performed by workers changed.\n\n## Occupational Exposure and Susceptibility to AI\n\nEarly foundational work on occupational susceptibility to automation estimated the probability of computerisation for 702 detailed occupations using a Gaussian process classifier, establishing a methodology for assessing how many jobs are at risk and how susceptibility relates to wages and educational attainment. The same study found that higher-income occupations exhibit a strong positive relationship between AI impact and both employment and wages, suggesting that access to complementary skills and technologies may play an important role and that AI has the potential to exacerbate labor market polarization. This Canadian study categorized workers into three groups: roughly 4.2 million employees (31%) in high-exposure, low-complementarity roles; about 3.9 million (29%) in high-exposure, high-complementarity roles; and approximately 5.4 million (40%) in low-exposure roles, a distribution that remained very similar between May 2016 and May 2021. The advent of large language models prompted Eloundou et al. to estimate that roughly 1.8% of jobs could have over half their tasks affected by LLMs with simple interfaces and general training, a share that jumps to just over 46% when accounting for current and likely future complementary software developments. These projected effects span all wage levels, with higher-income jobs potentially facing greater exposure to LLM capabilities, and the impacts are not restricted to industries with higher recent productivity growth. A systematic literature review synthesizing this body of research concludes that AI's impact on employment is multifaceted, with new opportunities arising from high-skilled job creation and rising demand for interdisciplinary talent on one hand, and challenges from low-skilled job replacement and employment structure polarization on the other. A related measure called the AI Occupational Impact (AIOI) was introduced, providing evidence that, on average, occupations impacted by AI experience a small but positive change in wages with no change in employment, though this positive wage correlation is driven primarily by occupations with higher software skill requirements.\n\n## Empirical Evidence on Employment Effects\n\nAcemoglu and Restrepo's foundational study of industrial robots in U.S. labor markets found that one more robot per thousand workers reduced the employment-to-population ratio by 0.2 percentage points and wages by 0.42%. Their broader analysis estimated that an additional robot per thousand workers reduced the employment-to-population ratio by 0.18–0.34 percentage points between 1990 and 2007, while earlier AI exposure showed no discernible impact on employment or wages during 2010–2018. Baseline difference-in-differences estimates confirmed a statistically significant negative effect on high-substitution occupations of −0.122 in the most restrictive specification. Image-based generative AI models produced qualitatively similar effects, with a 2.1% drop in monthly jobs and a 5.2% decrease in monthly compensation in affected occupations. Notably, higher freelancer quality did not moderate these adverse effects, and there was suggestive evidence that top freelancers were disproportionately affected. A survey-based study from Saudi Arabia involving 341 employees found that approximately 47% of employees work in jobs vulnerable to some degree of technological replacement, with this figure dropping to 15% when analyzing task-level rather than job-level replacement. The OECD Employment Outlook noted that only 3.2% of U.S. firms used AI between 2016 and 2018, with enterprise-level adoption ranging from 23% in Ireland to 3% in Hungary across European Union countries, suggesting that low penetration rates may partly explain the absence of large aggregate employment effects to date. Concurrent research found comparable magnitudes, with one study reporting a 3.87% reduction and another a 4.81% reduction in employment.\n\n## Productivity Effects of Generative AI\n\nExperimental evidence from a preregistered online experiment demonstrates that ChatGPT substantially raised productivity among college-educated professionals performing midlevel writing tasks, with one source reporting 453 participants while another reports 444 participants. The technology compressed the productivity distribution by benefiting low-ability workers more, thereby decreasing inequality between workers. ChatGPT primarily substituted for worker effort rather than complementing worker skills, restructuring tasks toward idea-generation and editing and away from rough-drafting. Workers exposed to ChatGPT during the experiment were 2 times as likely to report using it in their real job 2 weeks after the experiment and 1.6 times as likely 2 months after the experiment. Exposure to ChatGPT also increased job satisfaction and self-efficacy while heightening both concern and excitement about automation technologies. At a broader scale, a nationally representative U.S. survey conducted in August 2024 found that 28% of all workers used generative AI at work to some degree, with 9% using it every workday and 14% using it for at least one but not every workday. Among generative AI users, nearly one-third (31.9%) spent an hour or more per workday using it, while another 47.0% used it between 15 and 59 minutes daily. Time savings and usage varied considerably across occupations: workers in computer and mathematics used generative AI in nearly 12% of their work hours and saved 2.5% of work time, while personal service workers used it in only 1.3% of their work hours and saved only 0.4% of work time. One analysis found that across industries, information services had both the largest share of work hours spent using generative AI (14.0%) and the highest time savings (2.6%), while leisure, accommodation, and other services had the lowest usage (2.3%) and the lowest time savings (0.6%). However, formal firm adoption lagged behind individual worker adoption: only 5.4% of firms had formally adopted generative AI as of February 2024, suggesting that worker adoption remained mostly informal.\n\n## Skills, Polarization, and Work Transformation\n\nResearch using the AI Occupational Impact (AIOI) measure provides evidence that, on average, occupations impacted by AI experience a small but positive change in wages and no change in employment, though this positive wage correlation is driven primarily by occupations with higher software skill requirements. Higher-income occupations exhibit a strong positive relationship between AI impact and both employment and wages, suggesting that access to complementary skills and technologies plays an important role in determining outcomes and that AI has the potential to exacerbate labor market polarization. The interplay between machine and human comparative advantage allows computers to substitute for workers in performing routine, codifiable tasks while amplifying the comparative advantage of workers in supplying problem-solving skills, adaptability, and creativity. AI adoption necessitates a balanced approach to developing both hard and soft skills, ensuring individuals can effectively collaborate with and leverage AI technologies. The demand for skills driven by AI is moving towards higher levels of expertise and multidisciplinary integration. Among SMEs that use generative AI and have experienced a skill gap, 39% report that generative AI helped compensate for it, a figure rising to 46% where SMEs report improved employee performance. The International Labour Organization's 2018 publication on skills and the future of work in Asia and the Pacific emphasizes strategies for inclusive growth amid these transformations. Generative AI tools may also decrease performance inequality in the workplace, as one study found that benefits accrued most to the least experienced programmers, who completed tasks over 50% faster than those in the control group. The share of the employed population with AI skills is small—at most 0.3% of those employed in OECD countries, on average—but growing rapidly. Nearly half of the AI workforce in EU countries has labour earnings in the top two deciles of the labour earnings distribution. In the last few decades, one noticeable change has been \"polarization\" of the labor market, in which wage gains went disproportionately to those at the top and at the bottom of the income and skill distribution, not to those in the middle. Historically, professional, technical, and managerial employment grew by 3 percentage points per decade while agricultural employment declined by almost 4 percentage points per decade. A systematic literature review confirms that AI's impact on employment is multifaceted, with new high-skilled jobs and rising demand for interdisciplinary talent emerging alongside the replacement of low-skilled jobs and polarization of the employment structure. These findings collectively indicate that while AI is not yet causing widespread job displacement, it is reshaping task profiles and contributing to wage polarization between high- and low-skill workers.\n\n## Inequality and Distributional Consequences\n\nUrban workers face systematically higher exposure: all PUMAs in the top decile of AI exposure are located in metropolitan areas, whereas only 42.8 percent of PUMAs in the least exposed decile are in metro areas. However, the digital divide creates a critical asymmetry: workers in positions vulnerable to automation typically maintain sufficient internet connectivity to experience displacement, while those who could benefit from GenAI augmentation face substantial infrastructure gaps that may prevent productivity gains. In Mali, for example, just 12 percent of jobs are exposed to GenAI, but only 10 percent of those workers have internet access, meaning only 1.2 percent of workers hold jobs that are both exposed and practically usable with GenAI. By contrast, about one out of three workers in Switzerland and Singapore have jobs exposed to GenAI, and virtually all of them have internet access. Age disparities are pronounced in upper-middle-income countries, where approximately 10.4 percent of workers aged 16–35 face automation risk, while only 4.3 percent of workers aged 56–65 are in that position. Disparities in digital infrastructure suggest that developing countries may experience the disruptive effects of GenAI faster than its productivity benefits.\n\n## Cross-Study Synthesis and Contradictions\n\nIn contrast, several analyses using the Current Population Survey show \"at most small changes in hiring in AI-exposed jobs,\" and one review concludes that the evidence on how AI is affecting the labor market today is \"inconclusive\" and that claims about harmful impacts on particular groups are \"premature\". Concerns about inequality emerge across several analyses, with one noting that major AI developments remain concentrated in the hands of a few companies such as Microsoft, Google, Alibaba, and Tesla, potentially widening the income gap between those with and without advanced cognitive skills. When AI can perform most tasks comprising a job, the share of workers in that role within a firm falls by about 14%, but when AI's impact is concentrated in just a few tasks, employment in that role can actually grow. Legal roles gained the most from AI, with a predicted 6.4% increase in employment, because they face little direct automation impact and are often situated within firms that use AI heavily. Despite these specific findings, one analysis concludes that the overall evidence on how AI is affecting the labor market today is inconclusive, and claims about harmful impacts on particular groups of workers are premature. That analysis identifies three reasons for this uncertainty: early research findings on labor demand are collectively inconclusive, current data necessarily provide weak signals about future effects, and existing research on labor demand represents only one corner of the vast landscape of urgent AI research questions. A separate measure based on Anthropic's Claude chatbot data estimates that only 6.1% of queries can be associated with tasks performed by computer programmers, highlighting substantial variation depending on the measurement framework employed. Most economic experts believe the implications of AI for global economic growth are extremely uncertain, rendering forecasting impossible, though one estimate suggests AI could boost global growth by approximately 0.5 percentage points. Different methodological approaches yield divergent exposure estimates: one approach assigns computer programmers an exposure score of 1.272 versus 0.034 for manicurists. AI usage improves cover letters but makes them less informative signals of worker ability, leading employers to shift toward alternative signals such as past reviews when evaluating candidates, which can make it harder to form good matches due to diluted labor market signals. Without deliberate policy interventions, workers might not share in the gains from technological change through AI, as the overall impact will come from the balance of potential benefits and harms felt across tasks, skills, occupations, people, places, and time.\n\n## Limitations\n\nLimitations: The corpus is heavily skewed toward secondary and grey-literature tiers, with only 6% of sources classified as T1 primary studies and just 8% as T2, while T4 and T6 each account for 21% — meaning nearly half of all sources are downstream syntheses or non-peer-reviewed materials whose claims depend on earlier work that may itself be under-represented. These developments have intensified concerns about technological unemployment, prompting a growing body of research on AI-induced labor displacement, skill polarization, and industry-specific disruptions in sectors such as healthcare, education, and creative industries. Within this framework, the net impact of automation on labor demand depends on the balance between a displacement effect, which reduces the labor share of value added, and a productivity effect, which raises demand for labor in non-automated tasks. Notably, even workers in high-wage roles heavily exposed to AI saw their share of total employment grow by about 3% over five years, because AI boosted firm productivity and companies that used the technology grew faster. This pattern diverges from earlier technology waves—such as computerization and factory automation from the 1980s through the early 2000s—which mainly displaced middle-skill, routine jobs like clerical work and basic bookkeeping. Consistent with this divergence, one study employing a novel text-based method finds that, in contrast to software and robots, AI is directed at high-skilled tasks and estimates that AI will reduce 90:10 wage inequality without affecting the top 1%. The same study found that retail sales jobs dropped from 7.5 to 5.7 percent of the job market between 2013 and 2023, a reduction of 25 percent, with the e-commerce sector noted as an early adopter of predictive AI. The scope of this review encompasses peer-reviewed literature drawn from multiple databases, including a systematic literature review guided by the PRISMA framework covering Scopus publications from 2015 to July 2025, and a bibliometric analysis of Web of Science records from 2011 to 2020 that yielded 628 records, from which 108 non-English studies were excluded and 40 were included in qualitative assessment. Taken together, the evidence to be examined converges on three dominant themes: trends in AI-induced labor displacement and task automation, the adverse effects on white-collar professionals and gig workers through increased precarity and skill mismatches, and the fragmented mitigation strategies aimed at balancing technological advancement with employment protection. The concept of Industry 4.0 encompasses automation, robotics, artificial intelligence, and the Internet of Things in services and manufacturing, blurring the boundaries of the real world and virtual reality through cyber–physical production systems. A foundational task-based framework, building on prior work by Acemoglu and Restrepo (2018) as well as Autor, Levy, and Murnane (2003), conceptualizes how new technologies impact the allocation of tasks between capital and labor. Nevertheless, the task content of production has shifted significantly against labor over recent decades, with stronger displacement effects and considerably weaker reinstatement effects estimated for the last three decades compared with earlier periods. Researchers analyzing occupational churn in the U.S. labor market over the last century found that the share of STEM jobs grew from 6.5 percent in 2010 to nearly 10 percent in 2024, an almost 50 percent increase.\n\n## Theoretical Frameworks: Displacement, Reinstatement, and Task-Based Models\n\nCounterbalancing this displacement, the reinstatement effect arises when new technologies create tasks in which labor holds a comparative advantage, thereby shifting the task content of production back in favor of labor. Empirical evidence from US labor markets underscores the materiality of these displacement dynamics: Acemoglu and Restrepo find that one additional industrial robot per thousand workers reduces the employment-to-population ratio by 0.2 percentage points and wages by 0.42% across commuting zones, with effects that are distinct from other capital deepening or technology shocks after 1990. Their findings reveal that AI innovations related to engagement, learning, or creativity tend to augment human labor, whereas perception-related AI innovations displace it, with augmenting AI raising firm-level productivity and displacing AI lowering operating costs. Notably, the realized value of these innovations depends critically on labor-market frictions, as augmenting AI yields more positive valuation effects when firms have better access to prospective hires, while displacing AI yields less positive effects when termination costs are high. The task-based framework formalized by Acemoglu and Restrepo provides a foundational lens for understanding how automation restructures labor demand by shifting the allocation of tasks between capital and labor. In this framework, automation corresponds to capital taking over tasks previously performed by labor, and the displacement effect implies that automation reduces the labor share of value added. The net impact of automation on labor demand thus depends on how the displacement and productivity effects balance. Building on earlier contributions by Autor, Levy, and Murnane (2003) and Zeira (1998), this task-based framework foregrounds changes in the task content of production as a distinct channel through which technology affects labor demand and productivity.\n\n## Occupational Exposure and Susceptibility to AI\n\nhodology to estimate the probability of computerisation for 702 detailed occupations, using a Gaussian process classifier. More recent work focusing specifically on large language models finds that roughly 1.8% of jobs could have over half their tasks affected by LLMs with simple interfaces and general training, but when accounting for complementary software developments this share rises to just over 46% of jobs. A text-mining analysis of the International Standard Occupation Classification reveals that 32.8% of occupations could be fully impacted by ChatGPT, while 36.5% might experience a partial impact and 30.7% are likely to remain unaffected. These findings represent potential implications rather than realized labor market effects, underscoring the need for continued tracking of actual employment patterns as generative AI adoption spreads. This task-centric approach reveals that AI affects not only routine tasks, as previously assumed, but also nonroutine ones such as diagnosing health conditions, programming computers, and tracking flight routes. Importantly, AI's impact on labor is limited by the fact that some affected occupations are augmented rather than replaced, including neurologists, software engineers, and air traffic controllers, and that several affected sectors—IT, Healthcare, and Transport—are simultaneously experiencing labor shortages. Overall exposure also varies by national income level, ranging from 11% of total employment in low-income countries to 34% in high-income countries. Clerical occupations continue to exhibit the highest exposure levels, while some strongly digitized occupations have seen increased exposure, reflecting the expanding abilities of GenAI regarding specialized tasks in professional and technical roles. A separate study introduces an AI Impact measure that leverages deep learning natural language processing to match 24,758 AI patents filed between 2015 and 2022 against 17,879 task descriptions, enabling task-level rather than occupation-level analysis. Because most occupations consist of tasks that require human input, the most likely impact of GenAI across these analyses is the transformation of jobs.\n\n## Productivity Effects of Generative AI\n\nIn a preregistered online experiment, 453 college-educated professionals were assigned occupation-specific, incentivized writing tasks, with half randomly exposed to ChatGPT. Results showed that ChatGPT substantially raised productivity: the average time taken decreased by 40% and output quality rose by 18%. In the treatment group, time taken on the post-treatment task dropped by 11 minutes, or 0.75 standard deviations, relative to the control group, who took an average of 27 minutes. Average evaluator grades increased by 0.45 standard deviations, with similar increases for overall grades and specific grades for writing quality, content quality, and originality. The intervention also reduced productivity inequality: the correlation between first-task and second-task grades fell from 0.41 in the control group to 0.14 in the treatment group, indicating that participants who scored lower initially benefited more from ChatGPT access. Workers exposed to ChatGPT during the experiment were 2 times as likely to report using it in their real job 2 weeks after the experiment and 1.6 times as likely 2 months after the experiment. These effects were statistically significant at the 1% level and operated on both the extensive and intensive margins: freelancers were 1.2% less likely to receive any employment in a given month and took 4.7% fewer jobs conditional on employment. The introduction of image-based generative AI models produced qualitatively similar effects, with a 2.1% reduction in the monthly number of jobs and a 5.2% decrease in total monthly compensation in affected occupations. Notably, the study finds suggestive evidence that top freelancers were disproportionately affected by AI, and no evidence that high-quality service, measured by past performance and employment, moderated the adverse effects. Together, these findings suggest that while generative AI can complement workers by enhancing productivity and reducing inequality among workers, it may also substitute for labor in certain occupations, reducing overall demand and transforming the role of human capital in production. Following the release of ChatGPT, freelancers in more affected occupations experienced a 2% decrease in the number of monthly jobs and a 5.2% decrease in monthly earnings on the platform compared with freelancers in less-affected occupations. These estimated effects are generally at least as large as those of other shocks to labor markets, with concurrent research reporting average reductions of 3.87% and 4.81% in similar settings.\n\n## Empirical Evidence on Employment and Wage Impacts\n\nCross-country evidence from 23 OECD countries reveals that, overall, there is no clear relationship between AI exposure and employment growth, though the effects differ markedly by occupational characteristics. The average AI exposure score across occupations ranges from 0.52 in Lithuania to 0.72 in Finland, while across the 36 occupations it spans from 0.26 for cleaners and helpers to 0.87 for business professionals. The share of job postings requiring AI skills rose from 0.10% in 2012 to 0.24% in 2019, although these figures remain very low as a proportion of total postings. A large increase in AI use is linked to about 6% higher employment growth and 9.5% more sales growth over five years. Specifically, a one-unit increase in AI is associated with a 0.063% increase in high-skilled employment and a 0.001% increase in medium-skilled employment, alongside a 0.001% decrease in low-skilled employment. Taken together, these studies converge on the finding that AI's employment and wage effects are highly contingent on digital skills, occupational task composition, and regional industrial structure, with complementary skills and technologies playing a decisive role in whether AI displaces or augments workers. AI applications are used across sectors to optimize workflow, automate routine tasks, and improve decision-making, reshaping not only how work is done but also what work is done. AI adoption necessitates a balanced approach to developing both hard and soft skills, ensuring individuals can effectively collaborate with and leverage AI technologies. Empirical evidence using a newly created AI Occupational Impact (AIOI) measure shows that, on average, occupations impacted by AI experience a small but positive change in wages, but no change in employment. These findings suggest that access to complementary skills and technologies may play an important role in determining the impact of AI, and that AI has the potential to exacerbate labor market polarization. Consistent with this, the share of the employed population with AI skills is small—at most 0.3% of those employed in OECD countries, on average—but growing rapidly, and nearly half the AI workforce has labour earnings in the top two deciles of the earnings distribution. The OECD finds that a one standard deviation increase in exposure to AI is associated with a 0.4 percentage point increase in wage growth, largely driven by occupations requiring a high level of familiarity with software. However, OECD case studies of AI implementation find that, so far, AI has led to little wage change, and men—particularly in finance—were more likely than women to expect a wage increase and less likely to expect a wage decrease, indicating AI may put further pressure on existing wage inequalities. Beyond wages, AI affects job quality through changes in the tasks workers perform: it can reduce tedious or dangerous tasks, but it may also leave workers with a higher-paced work environment. Algorithmic management supported by AI can lead to reductions in privacy and autonomy and increases in work intensity and stress for subordinates, even when those workers see little to no change in their own tasks. Notably, studies of generative AI applications show benefits accruing most to the least experienced programmers, suggesting such tools may decrease performance inequality in the workplace. AI also risks introducing or reinforcing systematic bias into a range of labour market decisions if not implemented with care, affecting workplace inclusiveness and fairness. The integration of AI even in specialized domains such as academic writing poses both opportunities and challenges in terms of ethics, quality, and intellectual autonomy. The concept of \"software 2.0\" suggests that AI, particularly neural networks, may replace human-performed programming. However, a shortage of digital skills persists in the Industry 4.0 era, and gaps between academic offerings and industry demands hinder workforce readiness. This positive correlation with wages is driven primarily by occupations with higher software skill requirements. Workers who use AI report higher enjoyment in their jobs. These findings underscore that skills-related challenges are important for managing AI's effects on workers and its adoption.\n\n## Cross-Study Synthesis and Contradictions\n\nAcross the literature, a consistent finding is that AI's impact on the labor market operates primarily at the task level rather than eliminating entire occupations. Historical evidence over the last two centuries reinforces this view, showing that technology has consistently replaced human labor in specific tasks without leading to widespread job loss. Multiple studies converge on the notion that AI tends to complement human workers by automating certain tasks, allowing employees to focus on activities where AI is less capable, such as critical thinking and idea generation. This contrasts sharply with earlier technology waves, such as computerization and factory automation in the 1980s through early 2000s, which mainly displaced middle-skill, routine jobs. Finally, while existing AI exposure measures have been validated against real-world outcomes such as Microsoft Copilot usage, researchers caution that these measures remain imperfect, and a separate stream of work on AI in academic writing highlights that integration poses both opportunities and challenges in terms of ethics, quality, and intellectual autonomy. Several analyses using the Current Population Survey show at most small changes in hiring in AI-exposed jobs. Yet the evidence on AI's effect on labor market matching is itself contradictory: one study shows that algorithmic writing assistance for resumes leads to causal increases in hiring and wages, while another finds that AI usage improves cover letters but renders them less informative signals of worker ability. Taken together, the evidence suggests that AI is affecting labor market outcomes in ways that vary across tasks, worker groups, and firm adoption patterns.\n\n## Conclusions and Research Gaps\n\nThe collective evidence on AI's restructuring impact on the labor market remains inconclusive, with one analysis characterizing the research as still in its earliest stages and arguing that claims about harmful impacts on particular groups of workers are premature. Early research findings focused on how AI affects labor demand are collectively inconclusive, and any research based on current data necessarily provides only weak signals about the future. A separate analysis confirmed similar results, reinforcing the interpretation that macroeconomic conditions rather than AI adoption may explain observed labor demand shifts. Despite this ambiguity, one paper provides some of the earliest large-scale evidence consistent with the hypothesis that the AI revolution is beginning to have a significant and disproportionate impact on entry-level workers in the American labor market. AI exposure measures from one influential 2024 study have shown very high correlation with actual Microsoft Copilot usage from 2025, suggesting that earlier predictions were largely borne out, though associating chatbot conversations with occupations remains challenging. The effects of AI on matching between employers and job candidates are similarly unclear: one study demonstrates that algorithmic writing assistance for resumes leads to causal increases in hiring and wages, while another finds that AI-assisted interviewing improves candidate selection. However, a countervailing finding highlights that when all resumes start to look alike, labor market signals become diluted, and one analysis indeed finds that AI usage improves cover letters but that cover letters subsequently became less informative signals of worker ability, prompting employers to shift toward alternative signals such as past reviews. Existing research on labor demand represents only one corner of the vast landscape of urgent AI research questions, and many plausible channels of impact on the labor market remain under-explored relative to labor demand. A critical unresolved question is whether AI will bring forth entirely new occupations or create new tasks within existing roles, since current papers naturally focus on occupations for which employment data and AI exposure measures already exist. There is an enormous opportunity and need for more research on the relationship between AI and the labor market, as well as better data, to address the mismatch between the supply and demand for evidence on this topic. One study found that job postings declined in 2022, prior to the public release of ChatGPT, and that this timing corresponds better to rising interest rates than to the launch of large language models.\n\n## Limitations\n\nLimitations: The corpus is heavily skewed toward secondary and tertiary sources, with only 6% of sources classified as T1 primary studies and just 8% as T2, while 21% fall into T4 and 21% into T6 categories, limiting the depth of first-hand experimental or observational evidence available for synthesis. Additionally, 15% of sources carry an UNKNOWN tier designation, meaning their evidentiary weight could not be programmatically verified, introducing uncertainty into any tier-weighted analysis. No contradictions_detected field was present in the pipeline telemetry, so no cross-source contradiction is asserted. The telemetry block provided no date-range field, so no explicit evidence-horizon boundary can be reported here; this absence itself limits the ability to characterize temporal coverage or recency gaps in the corpus."}
